I appreciate you not naming the nail salons. They are often run by recent immigrants, and there are many countries that don't have or enforce copyright laws, so it could be an honest mistake, instead of an intentional act of theft. I know the law doesn't care about that difference, but I do. Obviously, that's not an excuse for them to break the law, and if they keep using the image after someone tells them it's illegal that's really messed up. But I wouldn't want to see someone's small business get ruined, or their employees harassed, over a genuine mistake.

Itâ€™s not meant to be taken in a negative way. Tone and context can make a difference being used as a negative or positive way. Gringo(a): USA white trash (rough translation lol)  It's an insult used all across Latin America, both in Portuguese and Spanish. It is specifically for white people from the USA, usually used when they only know English, they aren't very cultured, and/or when they have blonde hair and blue eyes. Basically, when the person is very American, gringo is used to describe them.  Another use I've seen is when the person is latin or Hispanic, but they don't know Spanish and do more American than latin things. Obviously, the language or customs you follow don't determine how Hispanic you are in the slightest, but it's true that gringo is used in these cases.

I've seen people confused about the word "gringo" so here's a REALLY resumed explanation.   The word "gringo" comes from the Mexican soldiers from the past, and it was created in one of the fights with Americans for the Texas territory, the american soldiers used to say "green go" when they wanted the green soldiers to attack, and the mexican soldiers made fun of them by shouting "gringo" and also to distract them.  Even if it was created in mexico is a word commonly used in every Latino and Hispanic country and now gringo means a "white American" or "a person that is from an English speaking country" or a foreigner. Okay, so about the one in portuguese (it's portuguese, not spanish lol) says "The new trend amongst gringas is now here in Brazil!" The term gringa, at least here in Brazil, is just a term used for female forgneirs and tourists, while the term for male forgneirs is 'gringo'. It's mainly used for people who come from North America or Europe. The term itself has no negative conotation, but it *can* be used as a mocking term depending on the context. On this ad, however, the word is not used with mean intents at all, it's just saying how the nail product shown is a big trend amongst North American women.

Cristine it's not a spanish ad it's a brasilian one, it's portuguese! Gringo and gringa is a slang for "foreingner", so if you were here in brasil peope could say "Cristine is a foreingner" in portuguese "Cristine Ã© estrangeira" as they could say "Cristine is a gringa" in portuguese "Cristine Ã© gringa" and both means tha same, you are not from Brasil. And if you are interested in where do it come from: the word comes from the spanish word "griego" that means greek and was used because greek is very difficult to understand. That gave origin to the expression "talking greek" that means talking something incomprehensible, and then that was shortened to gringo... and here we are. I personally never saw someone using gringo or gringa in a offensive way, so I don't think that is the case, but yeh... it's strange.

We use the word Gringa as a  Derogatory term just like Gringo. We call the white man Gringo because at the border of Mexico leading into the US the white man/woman tells us "Green, go." And that is where the term has originated from but a common thing that Hispanics do in our language and grammar, is that we give nouns genders, so in the ad that said Gringa the "A" at the end would direct itself to a female while "O" will direct itself to a male. Think of "Nino" and "Nina". One means boy and one means girl only because of the "A" or "O" at the end. But we use Gringo and Gringa as an insult to white people.
The salons probably just google-image-searched some things to put up. That dip powder nails thumbnail that a bunch of them were using shows up when you google image search "dip powder nails." A lot of people, even the younger generations, don't know that the images from google aren't always free for public use. That doesn't make it okay, but I just thought I'd offer up a more understandable explanation than them just being shady.

*"A nova febre entre as gringas" is Portuguese, Cristine, not Spanish. Gringo/gringa is a slang word that can have different meanings depending on the country you're in. For example in Mexico, a gringo (male) or gringa (female) is a person from the United States, not ANY native English speaker. It's not mean, we call them that because they don't use their demonym Â«UsanianÂ» (United States). American is anyone from the American continent, you from Canada, me from Mexico all the way down to Brazil and Argentina. So when you say American you're not specifying any country, but if you say gringa you know that person is from the U.S.*

Gringo doesn't mean anything bad, it just depends how it's said like many other words. It's commonly used by Mexicans and other Latinos to refer to white people from the United States. Some will use it to refer to other English-speaking white people, but that would be incorrect, so in Mexico, we use "Blanco(a)" which means white. Gabacho(a) is also used in Mexico to refer to white people from the US or things from the US. Mexicans also call the US, "Gringolandia".  btw the ad was in Portuguese.

"Gringo" is an name Latinos use to refer to American people. It comes from back when American soldiers occupied Latin American land. The soldiers' uniforms were green and people use to yell at them "Green go home". So it evolved until it became "Gringo". So the root of the word is mean, but nowadays people use it to reefer to Americans and sometimes any other English speaking person (because all of that is "the same thing" ğŸ™„).

I wonder if the problem is with these websites that offer free clip art that you can download.  There are a lot of websites that are offering "public domain" images.  As far as I know a YouTube thumbnail photos do not fall under copyright or trademark due to the fact that the thumbnail is a snapshot of one second of video which I believe falls under the "fair use" rule.  That's why music teachers are able to use written music in bits and pieces in their copied notes for students.....which means the stealing is coming into the problem is because they are not pointing out that they are not blatantly informing the public who's images they are using.....which is even worse.  The fact that the one company left your watermark on the video means they purposely left it there.....the ones that blurred it......yeah......no.......really bad practice.........but "fair use" also means they had to at least inform you they wanted to use it....disgusting how some people and/or companies abuse the "fair use" rules.  Question: Is YouTube paying fees to trademark/copyright your materials for you?!  Are you paying for trademark/copyright for your content?  If not, unfortunately it's fair game for these companies to do what their doing if the content isn't trademarked/copyrighted.  There are many cases being thrown out by crafters because they are saying others are stealing their work to sell it, but did not take the steps to trademark their item so therefore it is open to bring copied and sold by who ever wants to........trademarks/copyrights is a very slippery slope (and confusing) when it comes to proving the "fair use" rule as well as other parts of it.

Re: Salons printing out thumbnails... coming from a graphics person, I totally get how this happens. Yeah, some might be deliberately ripping it off, but unfortunately, due to either/both lack of education or willful ignorance, there are many people in business that think it is completely acceptable to utilize Google image search as a free-for-all stock image catalog. They search "dip powder nails," find that thumbnail in one scroll, see the shiny image and the text, and in their minds, the ad is done! It goes to a designer or printer who doesn't know or care that it's not their content, gets a resolution boost, and gets printed. It's sad, but terribly common. I've had to explain too many times, "No, it's not free just because you got it from Google."

The text that says "a nove frebre entre as gringas, o alogamiento em polygel puede ser feito em casa, e promete unhas grandes e resistentes ate 20 dias" it's portuguese, "gringas" it's a despective nickname that people use referring to american, australian, canadian and british people, it's a composed work of "green" (because the US army used green regimentals back in the 1910's, when US tried to enter into Mexico between the mexican revolution) and "go". meantime, it's a classic meal on the north of the country,  technically a quesadilla with roast meat and salsa. As a translation, says "the new feber between the gringas, the polygel treatment can be done in home, promise long and strong nails during 20 days". hi, ok so the word "gringo/gringa" at least where I come from means anyone form the states, and it's not a slur or anything used to insult anyone normally. The story behind it (at least in my country) is when the US invaded, they wore green military uniforms. Since not many people knew how to speak english, and they wanted the military men to leave, they would yell "green go", as to telling them to leave. The name stuck so now we say gringo.

6:39 Sooooo.... first off not spanish, second off I'm Mexican and "gringa" means foreigner and is especially used for American girls. It was based off of when in war the Americans, to signal attack, would say "Green go!" because of the color of their uniforms. And since the spanish didn't understand, they heard "gringo" and used it to describe foreign (usually white) men,  and for women the term is "gringa". The word "gringo" meaning "green go" comes from protesters  of war torn countries by U. S military troops invading their territories during the 70's & 80's. Protesters would chant: greens go home. And the word gringo (slang) was born. Now the word is used either as term of endearment and or derogatory towards North American Caucasians.

Gringo is a mexican/latino racial slur towards foreigners, especially white people. Popular in the 40's-60's (don't @ me if the date is wrong) it can be featured in the Elvis Presley movie "Fun in Acapulco (1963)," similar in fashion to the way America called African Americans "Negros" in this same time period. While common place back then, it does not really fit into todays society of knowledge and acceptance of others no matter their race or orientation, though recent times have proved that we need to relearn that lesion. So yeah...... just a slur

Hi! "A nova febre entre as gringas" is actually Brazilian Portuguese. About the word "gringa", I saw comments saying that in latin american Spanish speaking countries, it is sometimes used in a mean way to say something like "white girl", but I also saw comments saying that in these countries it is used to refer to English speakers. In Brazil, where the text is from, I saw some people saying the same, that it is used to refer to americans or English speakers. Where I live, in the south of Brazil, when older people use this word, they usually refer to europeans (remember that Brazil is a giant country, so a word can be used in different ways depending on the place). Among younger people or on the internet, I usually see it being used to refer to foreigners in general, everybody that is not Brazilian. I think it can be used in a mean way, but as a native Portuguese speaker, considering the context, I don't think that's the case. So, I would simply translate it to foreigner, not in a bad way, and the whole setence can be like "the new trend among the foreigners". If a Brazilian eventually call you "gringo", don't worry, the person probably just means foreigner, not in an offensive way. Sorry if I got anything wrong, I tried to summarize the comments I saw about it, feel free to correct me or ad anything. By the way, so cool to see how many meanings the same word can have. Also, nice to see how many latin american holosexuals there are!

It's disgusting that there are so many fast fashion/nails companies out there, and so many well-known celebrities/clothing designers etc, that it can be almost impossible to know if any advertisement is at all real.  Two things you can do to make sure: 1. Check the reviews on independent websites, including places that tell you if it's a dodgy website, like TrustPilot/Web of Trust. Yes, reviews can be bought, but you can still usually get some coherent and genuine reviews out there. Especially on sites where it's not just a star rating system, but comments etc.  2. If a picture looks dodgy, do a reverse image search on Google. That should help show if it's another knock-off, or possibly even the original source of the stolen content.  Unfortunately that doesn't seem to work on instagram (I was trying to see if I could reverse image search one of Simply's instagram pics to see where it had been stolen to) or mobile phone a lot of the time, but at least try to have a look and a google before buying something from an ad. But it will show you dodgy places like Shein and Romwe.

Latin American here and native Spanish speaker: First, that ad was in Portuguese. Second, gringa or gringo is a slang term used for people living outside of Latin America. It is mainly used for white Americans, but it can be used for pretty much anyone whose mother tongue is not Spanish or Portuguese. It can be derogatory because it is mostly used to make fun of tourists and foreigners.

"Gringo" comes from "Green go". It is a slur that originated in the border between the US and Mexico and it refers to the color of the uniforms of border patrol officers. However it is used throughout Latin America to refer to any english-speaking and/or white foreign person.

So... The word "gringo" comes from the war between Mexico and US. Mexican used to tell US soldiers "green go home". That eventually became "green go" and then "gringo". In most parts of Latam you use it to refer to a US citizen or maybe to europeans (for example, people sometimes refer to germans as gringos too). But in Brazil (I just learned this lol) is used to name any foreign.  It can be used in an offensive way, but most of the time is just like a nickname.

6:02 hey Cristine, Brazilian viewer here, let me maybe give some clarity to this one. First in Brazil we donâ€™t speak Spanish, we speak Portuguese, (just a heads up, Brazilians tend to get really upset that very few foreigners know that we donâ€™t speak Spanish) The â€œA nova febre entre as gringasâ€ really translates to something like â€œThe newest trend among gringasâ€. Gringas isnâ€™t in it self an offensive word, itâ€™s just a slang term for foreigners, most commonly used for English speakers (gringas is used when talking about females, and gringos when talking about males or both). The formal term for it in Portuguese would be â€œestrangeirosâ€, but that takes too long to say, so in day to day convos, memes and (as you saw here) advertising we usually will use gringa. However, the term can have a pejorative meaning, it really depends on context. (JUST AS AN EXAMPLE, based on this vid Brazilians could imply that youâ€™re ignorant to other American cultures and realities by saying â€œoh look, another gringa that assumes that every country in Latin America speaks Spanish...). In the add I donâ€™t think they were trying to used you or your brand to sell their products, I think they used your face to reference a major foreign YouTuber who uses the same type of product that they were advertising in a way to promote it (thatâ€™s really not an uncommon tactic, basically say and show that someone who speaks English is using a product and people here tend to associate that with quality and innovation). Anyways, hope this gave some clarification, love you vids, keep up the good work! ğŸ˜‰
